  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer14093069779663549568">Set largely on the pages of a website where gay male escorts are reviewed by their clients, and told through the postings, emails, and conversations of several dozen unreliable narrators, The Sluts chronicles the evolution of one young escort's date with a satisfied client into a metafiction of pornography, lies, half-truths, and myth. Explicit, shocking, comical, and disp</span>
  <span id="freeText14093069779663549568" style="display:none">Set largely on the pages of a website where gay male escorts are reviewed by their clients, and told through the postings, emails, and conversations of several dozen unreliable narrators, The Sluts chronicles the evolution of one young escort's date with a satisfied client into a metafiction of pornography, lies, half-truths, and myth. Explicit, shocking, comical, and displaying the author's signature flair for blending structural complexity with direct, stylish, accessible language, The Sluts is Cooper's most transgressive novel since Frisk, and one of his most innovative works of fiction to date.</span>

          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: $('box').getStyle('z-index')})});
        } else {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });
      $('rating_details_tip').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

<div id="review_117917064" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/117917064" />
    <a title="brian   " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/193310-brian"><img alt="brian   " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353195524p2/193310.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/117917064?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="brian   " class="user" itemprop="url" name="brian   " href="/user/show/193310-brian">brian   </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer117917064" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13706598639143114788">back in the good 'ol days, when goodreads was a much smaller (and some would say better) place, the #1 top ranked reviewer was a woman called ginnie jones. she claimed to be a very old women in pasadena (her avatar remained a found image of a frazzled pencil-chomping booknerd) who spent all her time reading, reviewing, and tending to her very sick husband. ginnie was an elusive bird: she'd rarely comment on others' pages and would almost never answer private messages. but she was part of our lit</span>
  <span id="freeText13706598639143114788" style="display:none">back in the good 'ol days, when goodreads was a much smaller (and some would say better) place, the #1 top ranked reviewer was a woman called ginnie jones. she claimed to be a very old women in pasadena (her avatar remained a found image of a frazzled pencil-chomping booknerd) who spent all her time reading, reviewing, and tending to her very sick husband. ginnie was an elusive bird: she'd rarely comment on others' pages and would almost never answer private messages. but she was part of our little group and her erudition and breadth of knowledge was, well, unbelievable. she'd post several terrific reviews per week and in the following threads would discuss, elaborate, and accept heaps and heaps of praise and accolades. <br /><br />on a hunch, a certain goodreader googled a chunk of a ginnie review and discovered something: her review was a hybrid of two reviews previously published in separate obscure sources. and she wasn't the author of either of 'em. as this news percolated through the group, and people googled more and more, it was discovered that nearly every one of her reviews was plagiarized. and she was sneaky about it -- she'd change personal information, dates, locations, and any/all facts so as to make it seem that she was the author; and she'd cobble up to four reviews into one vote-drawing superreview. <br /><br />it all went public and a brutal controversy erupted. (yes, i understand how totally nerdy this all sounds) - a monster thread (at last count the messages were in the high 400s) exploded all over ginnie's, at the time, most recent review and then someone discovered the motherlode: a review which ginnie had plagiarized about a book on the ethics of plagiarism! amazing! so ginnie deleted the monster thread and a GR club was started in order to discuss different aspects of the controversy -- topics: 1. the nature of plagiarism 2. is ginnie real? 3. which articles were plagiarized from which sources? 4. is internet plagiarism on a social website as egregious/unethical as plagiarism in/of a written text? while there was much dumb anger and shit-tossing, conversations frequently dipped into genuinely interesting epistomelogical/existential riffs on identity, authenticity, reality... it was cool. <br /><br />a huge seam split up the middle of goodreads dividing those who felt that a) ginnie should be excused b/c she's old and lonely b) ginnie should be excused as online plagiarism of this nature doesn't matter, c) ginnie should be excused as she's obviously someone who needs vast amounts of votes and praise to fill whatever hole exists in her 'real' life, and d) those who felt betrayed, hurt, and kinda repulsed by what she did. and it wasn't pretty. name-calling. account deleting. friend kicking-offing. and, finally, intervention by the goodreads gods which resulted in ginnie's banishment from the site. <br /><br /><br />and then Garygate. 18 months later a figure emerged on goodreads who seemed too ridiculous to be true. gary carpetbombed everyone's threads with preposterously punctuated comments with such a sense of flamboyance, bravado, and hyperbole he makes me look like mike reynolds. no shit. his avatar (a guy standing in his bathroom staring directly into the camera) and profile information were carefully examined so as to determine if gary was simply an (extremely) enthusiastic booknut, or just some asshole having a laugh. and so, again, a monster thread and group (the 'who is gary? what does gary mean to you?' club) followed in which several gary related topics were discussed.<br /><br />and then it got weird when one of us changed our avatar to gary's and cloned his profile page. within 30 minutes there were 4 or 5 garys all claiming to be the 'real' gary. it was, at once, strange and confusing and funny and stupid and, yeah, a little bit profound.<br /><br />and exhilarating to watch as it unfolded in real time. hit refresh and you'd have a slew of comments from gary, gary clones, a woman claiming to know gary in real life (revealed to be another goodreader who had created a false identity), those who were trying to figure it all out, those who were just kicking back &amp; enjoying the show... and it was then i understood the nature of the internet. y'see, i was born in 1974 and remember using the Vic 20 and Commodore 64. i remember my dad coming home one night with the atari 2600 and playing Pong for weeks on end, my head exploded by the concept that i was controlling a jumble of pixels on a computer screen, that that white bar was me! and that 'i' was moving around to prevent some more pixels from flying past me. rudimentary, of course, but a big deal back them. <br /><br />i was a kid right before this crazy computer explosion and find myself too old for it not to blow my fucking mind, but not so young that i'm one of those geezers who just can't get the interwebs. but watching ginniegate and garygate unfold, i saw the internet for what it is (for me, at least): the ultimate postpostmodern detective story. had bolano or borges made it into the 21st century, surely they would've written tales of online espionage (borges nearly did with <i>the aleph</i>). to sit alone in a room, a cave, a plane, a bunker, a lighthouse, and watch a drama unfold in real time on a screen which simultaneously plugs in dozens of 'people' who could be anyone (and claim to be anyone else), anywhere, and could do anything...? amazing. (of course all this wonderful and mysterious online anonymity is soon coming to an end) <br /><br /><br />so <i>sluts</i>. a hybrid of borges and bruce labruce. a novel in the form of comments/reviews left on a male hustler website in which, through multiple narrators, we piece together the tragic, LMAO, and frequently frightening story of an underage junkie hustler called brad and the web of dementia that spins out as a result of his real-life and online exploits. we follow those who seek out the 'real' brad online and in the flesh, we read threats by brad's pimp/torturer 'brian', we track a journalist for a gay magazine who may or may not be himself, brad, and/or brian. people fall in and out; a webmaster frequently intervenes; people stalk brad's known hangouts and fuck him, beat him, cut him, impersonate him, castrate him (or do they?); an HIV+ porn star makes a brief appearance claiming to have fucked brad, to have killed brad, to be brad, and then is (possibly) murdered himself. there's a lot of pomo pyrotechnics here -- but cooper never shows his poker face, never winks an eye, never allows one of his unreliable narrators piled atop unreliable narrators to reveal the puppetmaster. it's all surface, it's all a game. and the game is perfectly played. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13706598639143114788" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8977915947700639045"><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380412525ra/811192.gif' width='340' height='140' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><p>This is not your father’s porn!</p><p>
  <i>If it’s not porn, is it erotica?</i>
</p><p>Hell, no. Nothing erotic about this one. Although, it does speak to the many —versions (sub-, perv-, King James, etc.)</p><p>Think of it as Neil Simon’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/805509.Murder_by_Death' title='Murder by Death by H.R.F. Keating' rel='nofollow noopener'>Murder by Death</a> meets de Sade’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6351885.The_120_Days_of_Sodom' title='The 120 Days of Sodom by Marquis de Sade' rel='nofollow noopener'>The 120 Days of Sodom</a> with a side of Delany’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/85865.Hogg' title='Hogg by Samuel R. Delany' rel='nofollow noopener'>Hogg</a>.</p><p>In excruciating detail, a host of unreliable narrators post reviews of a male hustler on an internet website and in discussion threads, evaluating his rendered services and their own basest desires, while</p></span>
  <span id="freeText8977915947700639045" style="display:none"><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380412525ra/811192.gif" width="340" height="140" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><p>This is not your father’s porn!</p><p>
  <i>If it’s not porn, is it erotica?</i>
</p><p>Hell, no. Nothing erotic about this one. Although, it does speak to the many —versions (sub-, perv-, King James, etc.)</p><p>Think of it as Neil Simon’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/805509.Murder_by_Death" title="Murder by Death by H.R.F. Keating" rel="nofollow noopener">Murder by Death</a> meets de Sade’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6351885.The_120_Days_of_Sodom" title="The 120 Days of Sodom by Marquis de Sade" rel="nofollow noopener">The 120 Days of Sodom</a> with a side of Delany’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/85865.Hogg" title="Hogg by Samuel R. Delany" rel="nofollow noopener">Hogg</a>.</p><p>In excruciating detail, a host of unreliable narrators post reviews of a male hustler on an internet website and in discussion threads, evaluating his rendered services and their own basest desires, while comments from others claiming to know the hustler (more or less, but mostly more, intimately) provide slightly variant perspectives on the character (including his own perspective)—all under the shadow of that peculiar realm, full of opportunity for deceit, that is the internet.</p><p>Years ago, when I first started learning my way around the internet, I found a (now-defunct) gay chat room where a bunch of guys would hang out, say the most outrageously funny things, and generally have a pretty good time while ignoring the occasional Private Message opportunities that would sometimes arise. It was a fun place to be, a no-holds-barred opportunity exchanging humor and, believe it or not, occasionally even talk about books. Then came the Drama. Then the exposure of lies. Then the website just went away, as had all but the die-hards who were there for something other than the one-handed typing. The good ol’ days.</p><p>That’s not what this book is. This is the underbelly of that world. The Private Messages writ large. Brutal. Self-serving and entirely one-sided. Nothing beautiful. Nothing insightful. Yet, Real in ways Westboro Baptist only imagines.</p><p>Four stars, not for the squeamish.</p></span>

<div id="review_670569669" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/670569669" />
    <a title="Megha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1727205-megha"><img alt="Megha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361664925p2/1727205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/670569669?book_show_action=true">Jul 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Megha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Megha" href="/user/show/1727205-megha">Megha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Megha by: </span>
      Ali
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1727205-megha?shelf=in-goodreaders-we-trust">in-goodreaders-we-trust</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1727205-megha?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer670569669" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9585058085782604308"><br/>Anyone remember this goodreader who would pop up on every which review and ask if the book had violence or sexually explicit content? Kiddo, if you are still lurking around, don't ;) read this book. It has everything you have been looking for.<br/><br/>I feel fairly comfortable in assuming that every time this goodreader sees someone reading, say, in a library or a coffee shop, he doesn't go up to them ask the same question. No. He would rather do so while hidden behind the curtain of internet. Social med</span>
  <span id="freeText9585058085782604308" style="display:none"><br>Anyone remember this goodreader who would pop up on every which review and ask if the book had violence or sexually explicit content? Kiddo, if you are still lurking around, don't ;) read this book. It has everything you have been looking for.<br><br>I feel fairly comfortable in assuming that every time this goodreader sees someone reading, say, in a library or a coffee shop, he doesn't go up to them ask the same question. No. He would rather do so while hidden behind the curtain of internet. Social media lets people share with hundreds of people at once. But hardly anyone would walk up in front of an auditorium full of people and show them an instagram of their lunch. On this website, we have came across just-for-fun sock puppets. There have also been instances where a profile was seemingly created for the sole purpose of posting rude comments on other's reviews. While they sit snugly at their keyboards they don't have to face any judgement or consequences in the same manner as an in-person interaction. You can break off the interaction at any point, you can block people and they cease to exist. Internet provides some degree of anonymity (despite how much one reveals about themselves) and facelessness (despite how many photos one posts).<br><br><b>The many faces on the web</b><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380429600i/860712.gif" width="200" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><a href="http://www.dennis-cooper.net/slutsartwork.htm" rel="nofollow noopener"> (More of 'The Sluts' gallery.)</a><br><br>In <i>The Sluts</i>, Cooper dives into one tiny corner of the internet. It is a forum hosting reviews and discussions on gay male escorts. The reviewers/participants freely speak of their basest S&amp;M fantasies and (truthfully?) claim to have lived them. They openly solicit escorts to maim and kill and make a snuff movie out of it. They promptly admit to having killed someone for a snuff video. They pile lies upon half-truths, contradict others, contradict themselves, admit to have posted under multiple names and false identities, sometimes even come back to say <i>oh btw, I was lying when I said such and such</i>. And the reader is left to wander around among this host of unreliable sources. The reader is constantly spun around, manipulated and confused. And there is no narrator in sight to show you the ropes.<br><br>The main character of the novel is a teenage escort named Brad. He becomes an object of obsession among the forum participants and is the topic of much of the discussion. The reader has to put together a picture of Brad largely on the basis of all the rumors, facts, half-baked stories floating around on the forum. Amidst all of the that, Brad remains something of a blur. Cooper had vaguely based Brad on a Czech porn star from the 90s named Peter Azur who had a cult following for a while. In Cooper's mind, Brad looks like some sort of an amalgamation of <a href="http://www.dennis-cooper.net/images/thebrads.jpg" rel="nofollow noopener">pictures such as these.</a><br><br>The biggest draw of the novel is certainly its unusual format. Cooper employs the concept very successfully, I would say. Around halfway it may look like the novel is just a one-trick pony, but Cooper does have some more cards up his sleeve which he will reveal eventually, and lead you to some conclusion. Very rarely do books toy around with the readers the way this one does.<br><br>Fortunately for us, a lot of the times we don't necessarily have to decide whether something we came across on the internet should be believed or not. We can take it at its face value and carry on. I came, I saw, I moved to the next tab.<br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="9585058085782604308" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2599113219" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1742420446447025723">Rating: 4.0/5.0<br/><br/>I can't remember who or which website recommended this book to me. All I can say that it is something very dark and very sleazy. I feel even saying this is an underestimation describing how dark this book is! I could not find a physical copy so luckily I could borrow a digital copy from Archive.org, but I was in the waiting list for a quite some time which I forgot completely about until I was notified that the book arrived in my inbox!<br/><br/>The structure of this book is really interest</span>
  <span id="freeText1742420446447025723" style="display:none">Rating: 4.0/5.0<br /><br />I can't remember who or which website recommended this book to me. All I can say that it is something very dark and very sleazy. I feel even saying this is an underestimation describing how dark this book is! I could not find a physical copy so luckily I could borrow a digital copy from Archive.org, but I was in the waiting list for a quite some time which I forgot completely about until I was notified that the book arrived in my inbox!<br /><br />The structure of this book is really interesting. The story is about a homosexual male escort and we get to know about him through an online message board of an escort website where reviewers write reviews about their experience with this escort named Brad. But of course, the story shows us how the internet is not a reliable place to know about someone. Lots of twists and turns in this book, it will make you question who is telling the truth or who is lying. This book is not meant to be read by everybody. The amount of gore, profanity and sexual content in it is beyond anything I have ever read. You will feel all the emotions, be it disgust, empathy, shock and all. Everything is mixed up. It feels like a different world to what we are living but it is not different, people just go on with their imaginations, desires, and fantasies because it is the internet. <br /><br />The book has a lot of trigger warnings be it suicide, prostitution, pedophilia, BDSM, Watersports etc. Makes it really hard to mention all of it. Looking for something sick? then this is your book. I asked for something dark and got something darker than dark! There are parts of this book that I liked and there are parts that made me very uncomfortable. I tend to like books that throw me out of my comfort zone which The Sluts did here. I have not come across anything like this before and I doubt that I will in the future.</span>

<div id="review_479614867" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Nate D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d"><img alt="Nate D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1203897989p2/406701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/479614867?book_show_action=true">Dec 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nate D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nate D" href="/user/show/406701-nate-d">Nate D</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">the guys hanging around outside Pumpers</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Nate D by: </span>
      A classified ad from box 157
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/406701-nate-d?shelf=new-narrative">new-narrative</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer479614867" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5411328881598127056"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/129722531' rel='nofollow noopener'>Adam's</a> review of this is already essentially perfect:<br/><blockquote>What if Borges took on Rashomon, but instead of the action happening in a grove, it went down on a message board dedicated to reviewing homosexual escorts?</blockquote><br/>This is almost Cooper's perfect form: the frame means he only has to write a series of escort pickups that convey the entire story (for better or worse depending on your desire to read such), but more interestingly the format entirely lends itself to unreliable narrators lying about eachothe</span>
  <span id="freeText5411328881598127056" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/129722531" rel="nofollow noopener">Adam's</a> review of this is already essentially perfect:<br /><blockquote>What if Borges took on Rashomon, but instead of the action happening in a grove, it went down on a message board dedicated to reviewing homosexual escorts?</blockquote><br />This is almost Cooper's perfect form: the frame means he only has to write a series of escort pickups that convey the entire story (for better or worse depending on your desire to read such), but more interestingly the format entirely lends itself to unreliable narrators lying about eachother, obfuscating, creating their own naratives, and otherwise allowing for a pretty lucid discussion of the power of fantasies vs. reality, of of the power of the unfulfilled over the fulfilled, of the fascination of stories and untruth and how this context warps and alters the events they touch, of the old sex/death equation that Cooper can't really avoid. Plus it's at turns almost noir-like, completely horrifying, and for at least one stretch completely heartbreaking.<br /><br />Usual Cooper-recommendation caveats apply: there's an unflinching intensity here, but as always he has larger goals than just shock and squeam, building up layers of discomfort only to pull out the rug and recast his significances at the exact point at which I'm really starting to wonder at my own willingness to keep on rapidly turning the pages. Because relentless as this may be, it's also never less than relentlessly entertaining. But more than that, too.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5411328881598127056" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <span id="freeText8452379027211140895" style="display:none">This is a work of demented genius.<br /><br />I've never read something so convoluted and complex—structurally immaculate, mesmerising, both difficult to put down and to keep reading.<br /><br />Cooper hones in on the seediest most depraved corner of the internet and fleshes out their darkest fantasies while, most disturbingly, never mentioning where these fantasies are coming from. Everyone within the universe of this book is simply in agreement about what they want to happen—and what they want is the most fucked up shit imaginable.<br /><br />Maybe a shame that it won't secure a wider audience on account of its content—but the content is also very much the point.</span>

<div id="review_52513519" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/52513519" />
    <a title="Imogen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33887-imogen"><img alt="Imogen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353870889p2/33887.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/52513519?book_show_action=true">Apr 13, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Imogen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Imogen" href="/user/show/33887-imogen">Imogen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer52513519" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer809195264090942394">Dennis Cooper is such a good writer that it looks like he's just saying some stuff to you, which is hard to pull off. I like the mood you get into in all of his books: these semi-reliable narrators, who tend to be pretty out of it, lazily unspooling a bunch of stuff that happened. Even when, like in this one, there's structural stuff happening, the structure (and the mutilation, and the murders and suicides and bleak gay mopiness and whatever other sensationalist stuff is happening) is secondary</span>
  <span id="freeText809195264090942394" style="display:none">Dennis Cooper is such a good writer that it looks like he's just saying some stuff to you, which is hard to pull off. I like the mood you get into in all of his books: these semi-reliable narrators, who tend to be pretty out of it, lazily unspooling a bunch of stuff that happened. Even when, like in this one, there's structural stuff happening, the structure (and the mutilation, and the murders and suicides and bleak gay mopiness and whatever other sensationalist stuff is happening) is secondary to that laconic atmosphere. It's why you can always read his books in a day. <br /><br />And it doesn't hurt that his books (when they aren't the horrible stupid abomination <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/37415.Their_Eyes_Were_Watching_God" title="Their Eyes Were Watching God by Zora Neale Hurston" rel="nofollow noopener">God Jr.</a>, which I hate hate) tend to be matter-of-factly describing things that tend not to be talked about in polite company-hustlers who want to die, tricks who are stoked about it, barebacking and bug-chasing. I don't know. It's inviting, and it feels nice for somebody to be talking about these things, even if it's not necessarily like 'you will not believe the shit I actually did.' Y'know? It's more like, isn't it interesting, the shit that's in our collectvive id?<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />If I may rhyme.</span>
  <a data-text-id="809195264090942394" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_155504164" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/155504164" />
    <a title="Joey Comeau" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4481154-joey-comeau"><img alt="Joey Comeau" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472392666p2/4481154.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/155504164?book_show_action=true">Mar 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joey Comeau" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joey Comeau" href="/user/show/4481154-joey-comeau">Joey Comeau</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer155504164" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12219235418514885090">Dennis Cooper's novel, The Sluts, opens with an online review of a hustler named "Brad" who has mental problems. A second review follows, and then a third. The novel is told through these reviews and through emails and posts on a website devoted to reviewing hustlers. The posts by these reviewers mix the empty, repeated, imitative language of pornography with a series of straightforward, honest sounding voices. And they lie. <br/><br/>They lie, and they admit to lying when they think it will help you beli</span>
  <span id="freeText12219235418514885090" style="display:none">Dennis Cooper's novel, The Sluts, opens with an online review of a hustler named "Brad" who has mental problems. A second review follows, and then a third. The novel is told through these reviews and through emails and posts on a website devoted to reviewing hustlers. The posts by these reviewers mix the empty, repeated, imitative language of pornography with a series of straightforward, honest sounding voices. And they lie. <br /><br />They lie, and they admit to lying when they think it will help you believe their next lie. The saga of "Brad" on this website gets stranger and stranger and it becomes clear that the reviewers are obsessed. They are writing themselves into the story. We only rarely hear from Brad himself, who might have a brain tumor, who might be fourteen or eighteen or something in between, who might be real. The story that you piece together conflicts with itself and sprawls. He's in prison. His boyfriend has hired him out for violent sex and a man pays to break his legs during the act. Another man pays to cut his face and murder him. Only, maybe not. <br /><br />In the end, what's real is unimportant. This is a novel about the reviewers themselves. It's about their obsessions and about their ability to live inside their own heads. The sex described is brutal and graphic and unreal and maybe none of it ever happens and maybe some of it does. In any case, The Sluts is good. It's interesting and perverted and boring and relentless and numbing and I felt like throwing the book across the room a dozen times in anger. This is a frustrating and worthwhile book about voyeurism and fantasy and you are a pervert for even reading a review about it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12219235418514885090" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_11734361" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/11734361" />
    <a title="Christopher" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/373447-christopher"><img alt="Christopher" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/11734361?book_show_action=true">Jan 05, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christopher" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christopher" href="/user/show/373447-christopher">Christopher</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/373447-christopher?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer11734361" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8865256643232428227">Cooper's material here -- violent intergenerational gay sex -- is not for every one. But The Sluts brilliantly captures the seductive dangers of online behavior -- how we get entangled in a pointless debates in the comments sections of a web site (or write reviews of books for an audience strangers, ostensibly to "connect," but perhaps really to broadcast a "literate" persona and have our superior tastes reflected back to us). <br/><br/>The Sluts is almost entirely told as "comments" on an online gay sex </span>
  <span id="freeText8865256643232428227" style="display:none">Cooper's material here -- violent intergenerational gay sex -- is not for every one. But The Sluts brilliantly captures the seductive dangers of online behavior -- how we get entangled in a pointless debates in the comments sections of a web site (or write reviews of books for an audience strangers, ostensibly to "connect," but perhaps really to broadcast a "literate" persona and have our superior tastes reflected back to us). <br /><br />The Sluts is almost entirely told as "comments" on an online gay sex bulletin board, and it's never clear who is the ultimate master of discourse (there is no fixed narrator in this e-pistolary novel). The ground is always shifting, truth is elusive. A young escort may be L.A.'s best submissive bottom, or may be a deranged drug addict. Most likely, he's the figment of a prankster's imagination. But by the time this realization dawns, the many "web authors" of this fiction want too badly to believe in him to let him die—or live. Trenchantly, Cooper links our cyber behavior and our sexual behavior: both are governed by fantasy selves and untruth. "Only connect?" As Lacan said, "There is no sexual relation," meaning that sex involves mutual misrecognition, a potentially deforming projection of our fantasies onto our sex partners that has nothing to do with connection. In cyberspace, where anonymity and persona-making flourish, those sexual projections are rendered even more virulent, dangerous, addictive.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8865256643232428227" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer279597553939791384">I'm not easily shocked, but there were a few points when I was reading this where I needed to skip a page. If you can handle depictions of extreme sadomasochistic sex involving potentially underage male prostitutes, you'll be rewarded with one of the most structurally-interesting novels I've ever come across.<br/><br/>This book is a modern take on the 19th-century epistolary novel (in which the story is told through a series of letters): here the story is largely told through postings to a website dedicat</span>
  <span id="freeText279597553939791384" style="display:none">I'm not easily shocked, but there were a few points when I was reading this where I needed to skip a page. If you can handle depictions of extreme sadomasochistic sex involving potentially underage male prostitutes, you'll be rewarded with one of the most structurally-interesting novels I've ever come across.<br /><br />This book is a modern take on the 19th-century epistolary novel (in which the story is told through a series of letters): here the story is largely told through postings to a website dedicated to reviews of male escorts. The story is thus told through a number of different narrators, all of whom are potentially unreliable due to the anonymous nature of the Internet (not to mention the fact that the hustlers and johns described in the reviews are not likely to use their real names). These narrators constantly question the veracity of each other's reviews and identities.<br /><br />The reviews focus on the ongoing mystery of Brad, the beguiling young hustler who might have a death wish, and Brian, the infatuated sugar daddy who might grant Brad's wish. But in the end, it is clear that the story is really about the men who find themselves drawn into the mystery through their online community.<br /><br />Matt Hlinak<br />Author of <a href="http://www.amazon.com/DoG-Matt-Hlinak/dp/0615700217/ref=sr_1_1?s=books&amp;ie=UTF8&amp;qid=1361197916&amp;sr=1-1&amp;keywords=dog+hlinak" rel="nofollow noopener">DoG</a></span>

<div id="review_216953944" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/216953944" />
    <a title="Ashmedai" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6488864-ashmedai"><img alt="Ashmedai" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1317334069p2/6488864.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/216953944?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ashmedai" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ashmedai" href="/user/show/6488864-ashmedai">Ashmedai</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer216953944" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4859882406407511088">The Sluts is a novel which is mainly set on a web site where gay male escorts are reviewed by their clients. It's a story told through these reviews, e-mails, telephone conversations and posts on web forums, which is exactly why I was ambivalent about starting this book. I found it hard to imagine how a novel could be written in that format and not become a disjointed mess. However, once I picked it up, I could barely put it back down, and was riveted from cover to cover. It's hands down one of </span>
  <span id="freeText4859882406407511088" style="display:none">The Sluts is a novel which is mainly set on a web site where gay male escorts are reviewed by their clients. It's a story told through these reviews, e-mails, telephone conversations and posts on web forums, which is exactly why I was ambivalent about starting this book. I found it hard to imagine how a novel could be written in that format and not become a disjointed mess. However, once I picked it up, I could barely put it back down, and was riveted from cover to cover. It's hands down one of the best contemporary novels I've ever read, and one that blows 99% of so-called "gay fiction" clear out of the water.<br /><br />The story opens up with a review of "Brad", an adrogynous child-hustler with obvious mental problems, according to his client. This review is followed by another, then another, as slowly the story of "Brad" becomes more and more bizarre - Brad, who may be mentally ill, drug addicted, or not; who may be fifteen or eighteen, have a brain tumor, or not. Or all or none of the above. Who may not even be real. Who, if he's real, may be under the wing of a psycho-sadistic pimp named Brian, who's apparently hiring him out for "the ultimate" satisfaction of pedophiles, extreme sadists and, ultimately, snuff, after there's nothing left of Brad to dismember. Or maybe not! One review or conversation verifies any or all of the above as truth, and is immediately contradicted by another. And most of it is lies. The only certain truth is that Brad and Brian become objects of obsession, almost iconized by this on-line community, as their saga continues and seems to reach a conclusion...which is immediately put into question again. <br /><br />I'd definitely recommend this book, but not to everyone. The sex and violence described in the story are graphic and extreme, the plot is sordid and very dark, but if that's your cup of tea as much as it's mine, you're in for a highlight and a treat with this brilliantly written book. Brutal as it is, the violence is never gratuitous, it's a book for intelligent readers and a glimpse into the depravity of the mind, nothing I'd categorize in the mindless splatter-genre. Beyond that, I can't praise it enough, it's truly a tour de force.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4859882406407511088" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText2804652951840922218" style="display:none">This book is a serious mind fu(k! I'm still not sure exactly what I just read! What I do know for sure though is that I was glued it to each page and had a hard time putting it down at night.<br /><br />Dennis Cooper is an extremely creative person! In THE SLUTS, Cooper has developed a complex tale by using what are supposed to be reviews on an escort review website, emails and fax correspondence. Each of the reviews were posts by Johns describing their experience with the main character/escort. These reviews were extremely explicit and graphic – this book is definitely not for the squeamish! The sexual encounters described were extremely depraved and disturbing including medical kink/torture, meth-fueled gang-bangs and everything in between. <br /><br />I read the paperback version of this book and occasionally wondered whether there was a need for editing or if the typos, punctuation errors and missing words were intentional (an attempt to make the posts and correspondence seem more realistic)? Regardless, the editing was not so bad that it detracted from how much I enjoyed this book. If you like m/m erotica and have a strong stomach, I highly recommend this book!</span>

<div id="review_2101312109" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2101312109" />
    <a title="Kyle Likens" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/60118664-kyle-likens"><img alt="Kyle Likens" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1550898524p2/60118664.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2101312109" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4430907638851103656">As someone who considers American Psycho to be their favorite novel, the Sluts by Dennis Cooper was a serious mindf***. <br/>Alternately disgusting and engrossing, haphazardly written and brilliantly constructed, I was astonished at the amount of intricate violence there was in this satire of gay culture. And this was absolutely satire, bordering on parody but the story was too compelling to put down. I give this 5 stars, but I'm not sure if I'll ever read it again.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_387656226" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/387656226" />
    <a title="Melissa Jackson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2314907-melissa-jackson"><img alt="Melissa Jackson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443107039p2/2314907.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/387656226?book_show_action=true">Aug 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa Jackson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa Jackson" href="/user/show/2314907-melissa-jackson">Melissa Jackson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer387656226" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3535155842105712960">I am a physical reader. I feel what I read. Always. This can be awesome and it can be taxing-- especially when reading a text like "The Sluts." As my status update indicated, I almost threw up twice while reading it, I had tears in my eyes during a specifically emotionally distressing scene, I lost my appetite while eating one of the most delicious carrot stews I've ever had... you get the idea. This book is revolting, incredibly violent and beyond sadistic. It is a sexual horror story. It is al</span>
  <span id="freeText3535155842105712960" style="display:none">I am a physical reader. I feel what I read. Always. This can be awesome and it can be taxing-- especially when reading a text like "The Sluts." As my status update indicated, I almost threw up twice while reading it, I had tears in my eyes during a specifically emotionally distressing scene, I lost my appetite while eating one of the most delicious carrot stews I've ever had... you get the idea. This book is revolting, incredibly violent and beyond sadistic. It is a sexual horror story. It is also brilliant, so incredibly brilliant. Cooper manages to take an online message board thread of gay snuff fantasy over a particular teenage prostitute and turn out a moral commentary on the virtualization of the modern world and its dehumanizing effect. By exploring the fetish, he removes the fantasy and leaves the reader with the absolutely terrifying reality of perceived sexual desire, internet culture and the human condition in the wake of mental illness, drug abuse and desperation. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3535155842105712960" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText7289437891962822130" style="display:none">The third book of the trip was the first one <br />bought on the road and by the time I started <br />reading Dennis Cooper's "The Sluts" Jet had <br />already finished it in two days flat. It took me <br />about four. I found this and one of Dennis <br />Coopers other books God Jnr in the excellent Gay <br />Book Store on Santa Monica Blvd. We hadn't <br />realised it was the gay book store when we <br />walked in enticed by the cool window.<br />Still Dennis Cooper is still one of the sickest <br />writers around and The Sluts doesn't <br />dissapppoint in that respect. The book goes through some of <br />the sickest most perverted sexual practises you <br />could imagine and some I don't think I could <br />have imagined.   function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('09LExb2zxIrBqMByziJ7DtK1Mz+BFgg0otIxg+/YKYs8vlzqynq6zcP2qvKJ8TwgmY2Ti3hEflYmbf0R/0HlSA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('c6Kh/6Ck6PY2HSQLQ1afqYlWRS+IQ5mhELfmSnBkGcWczjnQ122WsTRDTosEhdiHwm7lm3ER78OUCCrYYP3VBg==')})
  }

          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11162213684998190069">Dennis Cooper was born on January 10, 1953 and grew up in the Southern California cities of Covina and Arcadia. In 1976, he founded Little Caesar Magazine and Press, which he ran until 1982. In 1985, he moved to Amsterdam for two and a half years, where he began his ten year long project, The George Miles Cycle, an interconnected sequence of five novels that includes Closer, Frisk, Try, Guide, and</span>
  <span id="freeText11162213684998190069" style="display:none">Dennis Cooper was born on January 10, 1953 and grew up in the Southern California cities of Covina and Arcadia. In 1976, he founded Little Caesar Magazine and Press, which he ran until 1982. In 1985, he moved to Amsterdam for two and a half years, where he began his ten year long project, The George Miles Cycle, an interconnected sequence of five novels that includes Closer, Frisk, Try, Guide, and Period. His post-George Miles Cycle novels include My Loose Thread, The Sluts and his most recent novel, the highly acclaimed God, Jr. Other works include the short-story collection Wrong, a collection of poetry titled The Dream Police and All Ears: Cultural Cristicism, Essays and Obituaries. Dennis Cooper currently spends his time between Los Angeles and Paris. His latest work, a limited edition collection of new poems, titled The Weaklings is available now from Fanzine Press.</span>
